<p>One major limitation of use of the DAS28 score in clinical setting is low-grade synovitis may be missed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Other</dt>
<dd>Other tools to monitor remission in rheumatoid arthritis are: ACR-EULAR Provisional Definition of Remission of Rheumatoid arthritis, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Simplified_Disease_Activity_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Simplified Disease Activity Index (page does not exist)">Simplified Disease Activity Index</a> (SDAI) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Clinical_Disease_Activity_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Clinical Disease Activity Index (page does not exist)">Clinical Disease Activity Index</a> (CDAI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Some scores are also available that do not require the input from a healthcare professional and thus allow self-monitoring by the person, like HAQ-DI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is no known prevention for the condition other than the reduction of risk factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Healthline_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Healthline-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is no cure for RA, but treatments can improve symptoms and slow the progress of the disease. Disease-modifying treatment has the best results when it is started early and aggressively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACR2008_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACR2008-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The goals of treatment are to minimize symptoms such as pain and swelling, to prevent bone deformity (for example, bone erosions visible in X-rays), and to maintain day-to-day functioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wasserman_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wasserman-63">[63]</a></sup> This can often be achieved using two main classes of medications: analgesics such as NSAIDs, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disease-modifying_antirheumatic_drugs" title="Disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs">disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs</a> (DMARDs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICEguidelines_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICEguidelines-64">[64]</a></sup> RA should generally be treated with at least one specific anti-rheumatic medication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACR2008_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACR2008-62">[62]</a></sup> The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzodiazepines" title="Benzodiazepines">benzodiazepines</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Diazepam" title="Diazepam">diazepam</a>) to treat the pain is not recommended as it does not appear to help and is associated with risks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesics" title="Analgesics">Analgesics</a>, other than NSAIDs, offer lesser, but some benefit with respect to pain whilst not causing the same level of gastrointestinal irritation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifestyle">Lifestyle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Lifestyle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Regular exercise is recommended as both safe and useful to maintain muscles strength and overall physical function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> It is uncertain if specific dietary measures have an effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Physical activity is beneficial for persons with Rheumatoid arthritis complaining of fatigue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Occupational therapy has a positive role to play in improving functional ability of persons with rheumatoid arthritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_modifying_agents">Disease modifying agents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Disease modifying agents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disease-modifying_antirheumatic_drugs" title="Disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs">Disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs</a> (DMARDs) are the primary treatment for RA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> They are a diverse collection of drugs, grouped by use and convention. They have been found to improve symptoms, decrease joint damage, and improve overall functional abilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> DMARDs should be started early in the disease as they result in disease remission in approximately half of people and improved outcomes overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The following drugs are considered as DMARDs: <a href="/wiki/Methotrexate" title="Methotrexate">methotrexate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxychloroquine" title="Hydroxychloroquine">hydroxychloroquine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfasalazine" title="Sulfasalazine">sulfasalazine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leflunomide" title="Leflunomide">leflunomide</a>, TNF-alpha inhibitors (<a href="/wiki/Certolizumab_pegol" title="Certolizumab pegol">certolizumab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infliximab" title="Infliximab">infliximab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Etanercept" title="Etanercept">etanercept</a>), <a href="/wiki/Abatacept" title="Abatacept">abatacept</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anakinra" title="Anakinra">anakinra</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rituximab" title="Rituximab">Rituximab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tocilizumab" title="Tocilizumab">tocilizumab</a> are monoclonal antibodies and are also DMARDs.</p>
<p>The most commonly used agent is methotrexate with other frequently used agents including sulfasalazine and leflunomide. <a href="/wiki/Sodium_aurothiomalate" title="Sodium aurothiomalate">Sodium aurothiomalate</a> (gold) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclosporin" title="Cyclosporin">cyclosporin</a> are less commonly used due to more common adverse effects. Agents may be used in combinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> Methotrexate is the most important and useful DMARD and is usually the first treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wasserman_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wasserman-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICEguidelines_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICEguidelines-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chapter94_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-chapter94-71">[71]</a></sup> Adverse effects should be monitored regularly with toxicity including gastrointestinal, hematologic, pulmonary, and hepatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chapter94_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-chapter94-71">[71]</a></sup> Side effects such as nausea, vomiting or abdominal pain can be reduced by taking folic acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The most common undesirable effect is that it increases liver enzymes in almost 15% of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chapter94_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-chapter94-71">[71]</a></sup> It is thus recommended that those who consistently demonstrate abnormal levels of liver enzymes or have a history of liver disease or alcohol use undergo liver biopsies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACR2002_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACR2002-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biological agents should generally only be used if methotrexate and other conventional agents are not effective after a trial of three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> They are associated with a higher rate of serious infections as compared to other DMARDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> These agents used to treat rheumatoid arthritis include: <a href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor_alpha" title="Tumor necrosis factor alpha">tumor necrosis factor alpha</a> (TNFα) blockers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Infliximab" title="Infliximab">infliximab</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interleukin_1" title="Interleukin 1">interleukin 1</a> blockers such as <a href="/wiki/Anakinra" title="Anakinra">anakinra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibody" title="Monoclonal antibody">monoclonal antibodies</a> against <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cells</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Rituximab" title="Rituximab">rituximab</a> and tocilizumab,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cell</a> costimulation blocker such as abatacept among others. They are often used in combination with either methotrexate or leflunomide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> In those who are well controlled on TNF blockers decreasing the dose does not appear to affect overall function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Persons should be screened for <a href="/wiki/Latent_tuberculosis" title="Latent tuberculosis">latent tuberculosis</a> before starting any <a href="/wiki/TNF_inhibitor" title="TNF inhibitor">TNF blockers</a> therapy to avoid reactivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGraw_Hill_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw_Hill-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>TNF blockers and methotrexate appear to have similar effectiveness when used alone and better results are obtained when used together. TNF blockers appear to have equivalent effectiveness with <a href="/wiki/Etanercept" title="Etanercept">etanercept</a> appearing to be the safest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Abatacept appears effective for RA with 20% more people improving with treatment than without but long term safety studies are yet unavailable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, there is a lack of evidence to distinguish between the biologics available for RA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Issues with the biologics include their high cost and association with infections including <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-inflammatory_agents">Anti-inflammatory agents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Anti-inflammatory agents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAIDs" title="NSAIDs">NSAIDs</a> reduce both pain and stiffness in those with RA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> Generally they appear to have no effect on people's long term disease course and thus are no longer first line agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> NSAIDs should be used with caution in those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_problem" title="Gastrointestinal problem">gastrointestinal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular" title="Cardiovascular">cardiovascular</a>, or kidney problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22141388_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22141388-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Use of methotrexate together with NSAIDS is safe, if adequate monitoring is done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/COX-2_inhibitor" title="COX-2 inhibitor">COX-2 inhibitors</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Celecoxib" title="Celecoxib">celecoxib</a>, and NSAIDs are equally effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Job2008_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Job2008-86">[86]</a></sup> They have a similar gastrointestinal risk as an NSAIDs plus a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_pump_inhibitor" title="Proton pump inhibitor">proton pump inhibitor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In the elderly there is less gastrointestinal intolerance to celecoxib than to NSAIDs alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> There however is an increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a> with COX-2 inhibitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Job2008_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Job2008-86">[86]</a></sup> Anti-ulcer medications are not recommended routinely but only in those high risk of gastrointestinal problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucocorticoids" title="Glucocorticoids">Glucocorticoids</a> can be used in the short term for flare-ups, while waiting for slow-onset drugs to take effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-15"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> Injection of glucocorticoids into individual joints is also effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-16"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> While long-term use reduces joint damage it also results in osteoporosis and susceptibility to infections, and thus is not recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-17"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In early phases of the disease, an arthroscopic or open <a href="/wiki/Synovectomy" title="Synovectomy">synovectomy</a> may be performed. It consists of the removal of the inflamed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synovia" title="Synovia">synovia</a> and prevents a quick destruction of the affected joints. Severely affected joints may require <a href="/wiki/Joint_replacement" title="Joint replacement">joint replacement</a> surgery, such as knee replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-18"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> Postoperatively, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiotherapy" title="Physiotherapy">physiotherapy</a> is always necessary.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Alternative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In general, there is not enough evidence to support any complementary health approaches for RA, with safety concerns for some of them. Some mind and body practices and dietary supplements may help people with symptoms and therefore may be beneficial additions to conventional treatments, but there is not enough evidence to draw conclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCCIH_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH-6">[6]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complementary_and_alternative_medicine" title="Complementary and alternative medicine">CAM</a> modalities (excluding fish oil) found that " The available evidence does not support their current use in the management of RA.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macfarlane_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macfarlane-90">[90]</a></sup> Studies showing beneficial effects in RA on a wide variety of CAM modalities are often affected by <a href="/wiki/Publication_bias" title="Publication bias">publication bias</a> and are generally not high quality evidence such as <a href="/wiki/Randomized_controlled_trial" title="Randomized controlled trial">randomized controlled trials</a> (RCTs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ef2010_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ef2010-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2005 Cochrane review states that <a href="/wiki/Low_level_laser_therapy" title="Low level laser therapy">low level laser therapy</a> can be tried to improve pain and morning stiffness due to rheumatoid arthritis as there are few side-effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is some evidence that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tai_Chi" title="Tai Chi">Tai Chi</a> improves the range of motion of a joint in persons with rheumatoid arthritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The evidence for acupuncture is inconclusive<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> with it appearing to be equivalent to sham acupuncture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_supplements">Dietary supplements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Dietary supplements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dt>Omega-3</dt>
<dd>Some evidence supports omega-3 fatty acids and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-linolenic_acid" title="Gamma-linolenic acid">gamma-linolenic acid</a> in RA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The benefit from omega-3 appears modest but consistent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> though the current evidence is not strong enough to determine that supplementation with <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid" title="Omega-3 fatty acid">omega-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids</a> (found in fish oil) is an effective treatment for RA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Gamma-linolenic acid, which may reduce pain, tender joint count and stiffness, is generally safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Herbal</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Rheumatology" title="American College of Rheumatology">American College of Rheumatology</a> states that no herbal medicines have health claims supported by high-quality evidence and thus they do not recommend their use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACRCAM_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACRCAM-99">[99]</a></sup> There is no scientific basis to suggest that herbal supplements advertised as "natural" are safer for use than conventional medications as both are chemicals. Herbal medications, although labelled "natural", may be toxic or fatal if consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACRCAM_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACRCAM-99">[99]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Due to the false belief that herbal supplements are always safe, there is sometimes a hesitancy to report their use which may increase the risk of adverse reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ef2010_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ef2010-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The following are under investigation for treatments for RA, based on preliminary promising results (not recommended for clinical use yet): <a href="/wiki/Boswellic_acid" title="Boswellic acid">boswellic acid</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Curcumin" title="Curcumin">curcumin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harpagophytum" title="Harpagophytum">devil's claw</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17627199_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17627199-103">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Euonymus_alatus" title="Euonymus alatus">Euonymus alatus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tripterygium_wilfordii" title="Tripterygium wilfordii">thunder god vine (<i>Tripterygium wilfordii</i>)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21365177_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21365177-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">NCCIH</a> has noted that, "In particular, the herb thunder god vine (Tripterygium wilfordii) can have serious side effects."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCCIH_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-NCCIH-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is conflicting evidence on the role of <a href="/wiki/Erythropoiesis" title="Erythropoiesis">erythropoiesis</a>-stimulating agents for treatment of anemia in persons with rheumatoid arthritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pregnancy">Pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>More than 75% of people with rheumatoid arthritis have symptoms improve during pregnancy but might have worsenings after delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGraw_Hill_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw_Hill-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Methotrexate" title="Methotrexate">Methotrexate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leflunomide" title="Leflunomide">leflunomide</a> are teratogenic (harmful to foetus) and not used in pregnancy. It is recommended women of childbearing age should use contraceptives to avoid pregnancy and to discontinue its use if pregnancy is planned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wasserman_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wasserman-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chapter94_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-chapter94-71">[71]</a></sup> Low dose of <a href="/wiki/Prednisolone" title="Prednisolone">prednisolone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxychloroquine" title="Hydroxychloroquine">hydroxychloroquine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfasalazine" title="Sulfasalazine">sulfasalazine</a> are considered safe in pregnant persons with rheumatoid arthritis.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccinations">Vaccinations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Vaccinations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>People with RA have an increased risk of infections and mortality and recommended vaccinations can reduce these risks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The inactivated <a href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">influenza vaccine</a> should be received annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pneumococcal_vaccine" title="Pneumococcal vaccine">pneumococcal vaccine</a> should be administered twice for people under the age 65 and once for those over 65.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Lastly, the live-attenuated <a href="/wiki/Zoster_vaccine" title="Zoster vaccine">zoster vaccine</a> should be administered once after the age 60, but is not recommended in people on a <a href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor_alpha" title="Tumor necrosis factor alpha">tumor necrosis factor alpha</a> blocker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/290px-Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/435px-Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/580px-Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">Disability-adjusted life year</a> for RA per 100,000 inhabitants in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b3b3b3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> no data</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff65; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> &lt;40</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fff200; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 40–50</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffdc00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 50–60</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffc600; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 60–70</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffb000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 70–80</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff9a00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 80–90</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff8400; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 90–100</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff6e00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 100–110</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff5800; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 110–120</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff4200; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 120–130</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff2c00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 130–140</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #cb0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> &gt;140</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The course of the disease varies greatly. Some people have mild short-term symptoms, but in most the disease is progressive for life. Around 20%–30% will have subcutaneous nodules (known as <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_nodule" title="Rheumatoid nodule">rheumatoid nodules</a>); this is associated with a poor prognosis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognostic_factors">Prognostic factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Prognostic factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Poor prognostic factors include,</p>
<ul>
<li>Persistent synovitis</li>
<li>Early erosive disease</li>
<li>Extra-articular findings (including subcutaneous rheumatoid nodules)</li>
<li>Positive serum RF findings</li>
<li>Positive serum anti-CCP autoantibodies</li>
<li>Carriership of HLA-DR4 "Shared Epitope" alleles</li>
<li>Family history of RA</li>
<li>Poor functional status</li>
<li>Socioeconomic factors</li>
<li>Elevated acute phase response (erythrocyte sedimentation rate [ESR], C-reactive protein [CRP])</li>
<li>Increased clinical severity.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mortality">Mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>RA reduces lifespan on average from three to twelve years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wasserman_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wasserman-63">[63]</a></sup> According to the UK's National Rheumatoid Arthritis Society, Young age at onset, long disease duration, the concurrent presence of other health problems (called co-morbidity), and characteristics of severe RA—such as poor functional ability or overall health status, a lot of joint damage on x-rays, the need for hospitalisation or involvement of organs other than the joints—have been shown to associate with higher mortality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Positive responses to treatment may indicate a better prognosis. A 2005 study by the <a href="/wiki/Mayo_Clinic" title="Mayo Clinic">Mayo Clinic</a> noted that RA sufferers suffer a doubled risk of heart disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> independent of other risk factors such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, alcohol abuse, and elevated <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a>, blood pressure and <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a>. The mechanism by which RA causes this increased risk remains unknown; the presence of chronic inflammation has been proposed as a contributing factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> It is possible that the use of new biologic drug therapies extend the lifespan of people with RA and reduce the risk and progression of atherosclerosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20678592_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20678592-115">[115]</a></sup> This is based on cohort and registry studies, and still remains hypothetical. It is still uncertain whether biologics improve vascular function in RA or not. There was an increase in total cholesterol and HDLc levels and no improvement of the atherogenic index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biologics_cv_effects.2F_cancer_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-biologics_cv_effects.2F_cancer-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/290px-Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/435px-Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/580px-Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rheumatoid_arthritis_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Deaths from rheumatoid arthritis per million persons in 2012
<div class="refbegin columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="-moz-column-count: 3; -webkit-column-count: 3; column-count: 3;">
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff20; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 0–0</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffe820; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 1–1</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffd820; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 2–3</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffc020; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 4–5</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffa020; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 6–6</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff9a20; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 7–8</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f08015; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 9–9</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #e06815; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 10–12</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d85010; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 13–20</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d02010; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 21–55</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>RA affects between 0.5 and 1% of adults in the developed world with between 5 and 50 per 100,000 people newly developing the condition each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2010_3-19"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2010-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2010 it resulted in about 49,000 deaths globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loz2012_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loz2012-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Onset is uncommon under the age of 15 and from then on the incidence rises with age until the age of 80. Women are affected three to five times as often as men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGraw_Hill_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw_Hill-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The age at which the disease most commonly starts is in women between 40 and 50 years of age, and for men somewhat later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> RA is a chronic disease, and although rarely, a spontaneous remission may occur, the natural course is almost invariably one of the persistent symptoms, waxing and waning in intensity, and a progressive deterioration of joint structures leading to deformations and disability.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first known traces of arthritis date back at least as far as 4500 BC. A text dated 123 AD first describes symptoms very similar to RA.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It was noted in skeletal remains of Native Americans found in Tennessee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In Europe, the disease is vanishingly rare before the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The first recognized description of RA in modern medicine was in 1800 by the French physician <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Jacob_Landr%C3%A9-Beauvais" title="Augustin Jacob Landré-Beauvais">Dr Augustin Jacob Landré-Beauvais</a> (1772–1840) who was based in the famed <a href="/wiki/Piti%C3%A9-Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re_Hospital" title="Pitié-Salpêtrière Hospital">Salpêtrière Hospital</a> in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landre1800_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Landre1800-8">[8]</a></sup> The name "rheumatoid arthritis" itself was coined in 1859 by British rheumatologist Dr <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Baring_Garrod" title="Alfred Baring Garrod">Alfred Baring Garrod</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>An anomaly has been noticed from the investigation of Pre-Columbian bones. The bones from the Tennessee site show no signs of tuberculosis even though it was prevalent at the time throughout the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14528501_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14528501-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The art of <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Peter Paul Rubens</a> may possibly depict the effects of RA. In his later paintings, his rendered hands show, in the opinion of some physicians, increasing deformity consistent with the symptoms of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7005475_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7005475-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> RA appears to some to have been depicted in 16th-century paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> However, it is generally recognized in art historical circles that the painting of hands in the 16th and 17th century followed certain stylized conventions, most clearly seen in the Mannerist movement. It was conventional, for instance, to show the upheld right hand of Christ in what now appears a deformed posture. These conventions are easily misinterpreted as portrayals of disease.</p>
<p>Historic treatments for RA have also included: <a href="/wiki/RICE_(medicine)" title="RICE (medicine)">rest, ice, compression and elevation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple" title="Apple">apple</a> diet, <a href="/wiki/Nutmeg" title="Nutmeg">nutmeg</a>, some light exercise every now and then, <a href="/wiki/Urtica" title="Urtica">nettles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bee</a> venom, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> bracelets, <a href="/wiki/Rhubarb_diet" title="Rhubarb diet">rhubarb diet</a>, extractions of teeth, <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electroconvulsive_therapy" title="Electroconvulsive therapy">electroconvulsive therapy</a> (ECT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Prosorba_column" title="Prosorba column">Prosorba column</a> blood filtering device (removing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IgG" title="IgG">IgG</a>) was approved by the FDA in 1999 for treatment of RA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> However it was discontinued at the end of 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rheumatoid arthritis is derived from the Greek word <i>ῥεύμα-rheuma (nom.), ῥεύματος-rheumatos (gen.)</i> ("flow, current"). The suffix -<i>oid</i> ("resembling") gives the translation as <i>joint inflammation that resembles rheumatic fever</i>. Rhuma which means watery discharge might refer to the fact that the joints are swollen or that the disease may be made worse by wet weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paget2002_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paget2002-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The cause of RA has been a subject of intensive research.</p>
<p>One theory is that infections may provoke the autoimmune response characteristic of rheumatoid arthritis. One example of this theory is that unique bacterial species associated with periodontitis may initiate an autoimmune response in genetically susceptible individuals by <a href="/wiki/Citrullination" title="Citrullination">citrullinating</a> self-proteins, thus leading to the production of ACPAs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Meta-analysis found an association between periodontal disease and RA, but the mechanism of this association remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D_deficiency" title="Vitamin D deficiency">Vitamin D deficiency</a> is more common in people with rheumatoid arthritis than in the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23370372_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23370372-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wen2011_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wen2011-134">[134]</a></sup> However, whether vitamin D deficiency is a cause or a consequence of the disease remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21067953_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21067953-135">[135]</a></sup> 1α,25-dihydroxyvitamin D3 (1,25D), an active metabolite of vitamin D, affects bone metabolism indirectly through control of calcium and phosphate homeostasis. Interaction between 1,25D and the vitamin D receptor (VDR) affects the production of RANKL and delays osteoclastogenesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Some trials have found a decreased risk for RA with vitamin D supplementation while others have not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wen2011_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wen2011-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rheumatoid_arthritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
